<<HEADING level=2>>Welding Processes, Inspection, and Metallurgy<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>1 Scope<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>This recommended practice (RP) provides guidance to the API authorized inspector on welding inspection as
encountered with fabrication and repair of refinery and chemical plant equipment and piping. This RP includes
descriptions of common welding processes, welding procedures, welder qualifications, metallurgical effects from
welding, and inspection techniques to aid the inspector in fulfilling their role implementing API 510, API 570, API 653
and API 582. The level of learning and training obtained from this document is not a replacement for the training and
experience required to be a certified welding inspector under one of the established welding certification programs
such as the American Welding Society (AWS) Certified Welding Inspector (CWI), or   Canadian and European
equivalent schemes such as CWB, CSWIP, PCN, or EFW.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>This RP does not require all welds to be inspected; nor does it require welds to be inspected to specific techniques
and extent. Welds selected for inspection, and the appropriate inspection techniques, should be determined by the
welding inspectors, engineers, or other responsible personnel using the applicable code or standard. The importance,
difficulty, and problems that could be encountered during welding should be considered by all involved. A welding
engineer should be consulted on any critical, specialized or complex welding issues.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>3 Terms, Definitions, and Acronyms<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1 Terms and Definitions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>For the purposes of this document, the following definitions apply.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.1
ACFM
The alternating current field measurement (ACFM) method is an electromagnetic inspection technique which can be
used to detect and size surface breaking (or in some cases near surface) defects in both magnetic and non-magnetic
materials.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.2
actual throat
The shortest distance between the weld root and the face of a fillet weld.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.3
air carbon arc cutting
CAC-A
A carbon arc cutting process variation that removes molten metal with a jet of air.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.4
arc blow
The deflection of an arc from its normal path because of magnetic forces.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.5
arc length
The distance from the tip of the welding electrode to the adjacent surface of the weld pool.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.6
arc strike
A discontinuity resulting from an arc, consisting of any localized remelted metal, heat-affected metal, or change in the
surface profile of any metal object.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.7
arc welding
AW
A group of welding processes that produces coalescence of work pieces by heating them with an arc. The processes
are used with or without the application of pressure and with or without filler metal.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.8
autogenous weld
A fusion weld made without filler metal.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.9
back-gouging
The removal of weld metal and base metal from the weld root side of a welded joint to facilitate complete fusion and
complete joint penetration upon subsequent welding from that side.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.10
backing
A material or device placed against the back-side of the joint, or at both sides of a weld in welding, to support and
retain molten weld metal.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.11
base metal
The metal to be welded, often called parent metal.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.12
bevel angle
The angle between the bevel of a joint member and a plane perpendicular to the surface of the member.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.13
burn-through
A term for excessive visible root reinforcement in a joint welded from one side or a hole through the root bead.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_12>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4
API R ECOMMENDED  P RACTICE  577<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.14
buttering
One or more layers of deposited weld metal on the face of a weld preparation or surface that will be part of a welded
joint.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.15
constant current power supply
cc
An arc welding power source with a volt-ampere relationship yielding a small welding current change from a large arc
voltage change.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.16
constant voltage power supply
cv
An arc welding power source with a volt-ampere relationship yielding a large welding current change from a small
voltage change.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.17
corrosion specialist
A person, acceptable to the owner/user, who has knowledge and experience in corrosion damage mechanisms,
metallurgy, materials selection, and corrosion monitoring techniques.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.18
crack
A fracture type discontinuity characterized by a sharp tip and high ratio of length and width to opening displacement.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.19
defect
A discontinuity or discontinuities that by nature or accumulated effect render a part or product unable to meet
minimum applicable acceptance standards or specifications (e.g. total crack length). The term designates rejectability.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.20
direct current electrode negative
DCEN
The arrangement of direct current arc welding leads in which the electrode is the negative pole and workpiece is the
positive pole of the welding arc. Commonly known as straight polarity.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.21
direct current electrode positive
DCEP
The arrangement of direct current arc welding leads in which the electrode is the positive pole and the work piece is
the negative pole of the welding arc. Commonly known as reverse polarity.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.22
discontinuity
An interruption of the typical structure of a material, such as a lack of homogeneity in its mechanical, metallurgical, or
physical characteristics. A discontinuity is not necessarily a defect.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.23
distortion
The change in shape or dimensions, temporary or permanent, of a part as a result of heating or welding.<<HEADING_END>>

5<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.24
Eddy current examination technique
ET
An inspection method that applies primarily to non-ferromagnetic materials.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.25
examiner
A person who assists the inspector by performing specific nondestructive examination (NDE) on components but
does not evaluate the results of those examinations in accordance with the appropriate inspection Code, unless
specifically trained and authorized to do so by the owner or user<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.26
filler metal
The metal or alloy to be added in making a welded joint.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.27
fillet weld size
For equal leg fillet welds, the leg lengths of the largest isosceles right triangle that can be inscribed within the fillet
weld cross section.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.28
fusion line
The interface between the base and weld metal.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.29
groove angle
The total included angle of the groove between work pieces.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.30
heat affected zone
HAZ
The portion of the base metal whose mechanical properties or microstructure have been altered by the heat of
welding or thermal cutting.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.31
heat input
The energy supplied by the welding arc to the work piece. Heat input is calculated as follows:
heat input = ( V   ×   i   ×  60) / (1000  ×   v ) in kJ/in., where  V  = voltage,  i  = amperage,  v  = weld travel speed (in./min).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.32
hot cracking
Cracking formed at temperatures near the completion of solidification.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.33
inclusion
Entrapped foreign solid material, such as slag, flux, tungsten, or oxide.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.34
incomplete fusion
A weld discontinuity in which complete coalescence did not occur between weld metal and fusion faces or adjoining
weld beads.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_14>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>6

<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.35
incomplete joint penetration
A joint root condition in a groove weld in which weld metal does not extend through the joint thickness.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.36
indication
A signal of discontinuity in the material under nondestructive examination.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.37
inspector
An individual who is qualified and certified to perform inspections under the proper inspection code or who holds a
valid and current National Board Commission.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.38
interpass temperature, welding
In a multipass weld, the lowest temperature of the deposited weld metal before the next weld pass is started.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.39
Image quality indicator
IQI
“Penetrameter” is another common term for IQI.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.40
joint penetration
The distance the weld metal extends from the weld face into a joint, exclusive of weld reinforcement.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.41
joint type
A weld joint classification based on five basic joint configurations such as a butt joint, corner joint, edge joint, lap joint,
and t-joint.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.42
lack of fusion
LOF
A non-standard term indicating a weld discontinuity in which fusion did not occur between weld metal and fusion faces
or  ad joining weld beads.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.43
lamellar tear
A subsurface terrace and step-like crack in the base metal with a basic orientation parallel to the wrought surface
caused by tensile stresses in the through-thickness direction of the base metal weakened by the presence of small
dispersed, planar shaped, nonmetallic inclusions parallel to the metal surface.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.44
lamination
A type of discontinuity with separation or weakness generally aligned parallel to the worked surface of a metal.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.45
linear discontinuity
A discontinuity with a length that is substantially greater than its width.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.46
longitudinal crack
A crack with its major axis orientation approximately parallel to the weld axis.<<HEADING_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.47
nondestructive examination
NDE
The act of determining the suitability of some material or component for its intended purpose using techniques that do
not affect its serviceability.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.48
overlap
The protrusion of weld metal beyond the weld toe or weld root.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.49
oxyacetylene cutting
OFA
An oxygen fuel gas cutting process variation that uses acetylene as the fuel gas.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.50
peening
The mechanical working of metals using impact blows.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.51
penetrameter
Old terminology for IQI still in use today but not recognized by the codes and standards.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.52
porosity
Cavity-type discontinuities formed by gas entrapment during solidification or in thermal spray deposit.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.53
positive material identification testing
PMI testing
Any physical evaluation or test of a material to confirm that the material which has been or will be placed into service
is consistent with the selected or specified alloy material designated by the owner/user.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.54
preheat
Metal temperature value achieved in a base metal or substrate prior to initiating the thermal operations. Also equal to
the minimum interpass temperature.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.55
procedure qualification record
PQR
A record of the welding data and variables used to weld a test coupon and the test results used to qualify the welding
procedure.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.56
recordable indication
Recording on a data sheet of an indication or condition that does not necessarily exceed the rejection criteria but in
terms of code, contract or is documented.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.57
reportable indication
Recording on a data sheet of an indication that exceeds the reject flaw size criteria and needs not only
documentation, but also notification to the appropriate authority to be corrected. All reportable indications are
recordable indications but not vice-versa.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.58
root face
The portion of the groove face within the joint root.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.59
root opening
A separation or gap at the joint root between the work pieces.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.60
shielding gas
Protective gas used to prevent or reduce atmospheric contamination.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.61
slag
A nonmetallic product resulting from the mutual dissolution of flux and nonmetallic impurities in some welding and
brazing processes.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.62
slag inclusion
A discontinuity consisting of slag entrapped in the weld metal or at the weld interface.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.63
spatter
The metal particles expelled during fusion welding that do not form a part of the weld.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.64
tack weld
A weld made to hold the parts of a weldment in proper alignment until the final welds are made.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.65
temper bead welding
A welding technique where the heat and placement of weld passes in deposited weld layers is controlled so that
sufficient heat is provided to temper each previously deposited weld layer.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.66
throat theoretical
The distance from the beginning of the joint root perpendicular to the hypotenuse of the largest right triangle that can
be inscribed within the cross-section of a fillet weld. This dimension is based on the assumption that the root opening
is equal to zero<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.67
t ra nsverse crack
A crack with its major axis oriented approximately perpendicular to the weld axis.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.68
travel angle
The angle less than 90 degrees between the electrode axis and a line perpendicular to the weld axis, in a plane
determined by the electrode axis and the weld axis.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.69
tungsten inclusion
A discontinuity consisting of tungsten entrapped in weld metal.<<HEADING_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.70
undercut
A groove melted into the base metal adjacent to the weld toe or weld root and left unfilled by weld metal.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.71
underfill
A condition in which the weld joint is incompletely filled when compared to the intended design.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.72
welder
A person who performs a manual or semiautomatic welding operation.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.73
welder certification
Written verification that a welder has produced welds meeting a prescribed standard of welder performance.<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.74
welder performance qualification
WPQ
A test administered to a welder to demonstrate the welder’s ability to produce welds meeting prescribed standards.
Welding performance qualification tests are specific to a WPS.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3.1.75
welding
A joining process that produces coalescence of base metals by heating them to the welding temperature, with or
without the application of pressure or by the application of pressure alone, and with or without the use of filler metal.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.76
welding engineer
An individual who holds an engineering degree and is knowledgeable and experienced in the engineering disciplines
associated with welding.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.77
welding operator
A person who operates automatic welding equipment.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.78
welding procedure specification
WPS
A document that describes how welding is to be carried out in production.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.79
weldment
An assembly whose component parts are joined by welding.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.80
weld joint
The junction of members or the edges of members which are to be joined or have been joined by welding.<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>3.1.81
weld reinforcement
Weld metal in excess of the quantity required to fill a joint.<<HEADING_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>4 Welding Inspection<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Welding inspection is a critical part of an overall weld quality assurance program. Welding inspection includes much
more than just the non-destructive examination of the completed weld. Many other issues are important, such as
review of specifications, joint design, cleaning procedures, and welding procedures. Welder qualifications should be
performed to better assure the weldment performs properly in service.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Welding inspection activities can be separated into three stages corresponding to the welding work process.
Inspectors should perform specific tasks prior to welding, during welding and upon completion of welding, although it
is usually not necessary to inspect every weld.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2 Tasks Prior to Welding<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The importance of tasks in the planning and weld preparation stage should not be understated. Many welding
problems can be avoided during this stage when it is easier to make changes and corrections, rather than after the
welding is in progress or completed. Such tasks may include.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.1 Drawings, Codes, and Standards<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Review drawings, standards, codes, and specifications to both understand the requirements for the weldment and
identify any inconsistencies.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.1.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Items that should be reviewed on drawings, Codes, and Standards include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Welding symbols and weld sizes clearly specified (see Annex A).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Weld joint designs and dimensions clearly specified (see Annex A).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Weld maps identify the welding procedure specification (WPS) to be used for specific weld joints.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Dimensions detailed and potential for distortion addressed.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_19>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>W ELDING  P ROCESSES , I NSPECTION ,  AND  M ETALLURGY
11<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Welding consumables specified (see 7.3, 7.4, 7.6, and Annex D).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>f)
Proper handling of consumables, if any, identified (see 7.7).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>g) Base material requirements specified (such as the use of impact tested materials where notch ductility is a
requirement in low temperature service).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>h) Mechanical properties and required testing identified (see 10.4).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>i)
Weather protection and wind break requirements defined.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>j)
Preheat requirements and acceptable preheat methods defined (see 10.5).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>k) Postweld heat treatment (PWHT) requirements and acceptable PWHT method defined (see 10.6).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>l)
Inspection hold-points and NDE requirements defined (see Section 9).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>m) Additional requirements, such as production weld coupons, clearly specified.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>n) Pressure testing requirements, if any, clearly specified (see 9.11).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.1.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review on drawings, Codes, and Standards include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Identify and clarify missing details and information.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Identify and clarify missing weld sizes, dimensions, tests, and any additional requirements.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Identify and clarify inconsistencies with standards, codes, and specification requirements.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Highlight potential weld problems not addressed in the design.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Establish applicable accept/reject criteria.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>f)
Verify that the appropriate degree of NDE has been specified.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.2 Weldment Requirements<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Review requirements for the weldment with the personnel involved with executing the work such as the design
engineer, welding engineer, welding organization and inspection organization.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.2.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Welding requirement quality control items that should be reviewed are as follows.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Competency of welding organization to perform welding activities in accordance with codes, standards, and
specifications.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Competency of inspection organization to perform specified inspection tasks.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Roles and responsibilities of engineers, welding organization, and welding inspectors defined and appropriate for
the work.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_20>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>12
API R ECOMMENDED  P RACTICE  577<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Independence of the inspection organization from the production organization is clear and demonstrated.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Competency of welding organization to perform welder/welding operator qualifications.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.2.2 Potential Inspector Action<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Highlight deficiencies and concerns with the organizations to appropriate personnel.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.3 Procedures and Qualification Records<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Review the WPS(s) and welder performance qualification record(s) (WPQ) to assure they are acceptable for the
work.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.3.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Welding procedure and qualification records that should be reviewed include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) WPS(s), including those developed for making repairs, are properly qualified and meet applicable codes,
standards and specifications for the work (see 6.4).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Procedure qualification records (PQR) are properly performed and support the WPS(s) (see 6.4).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Welder performance qualifications (WPQ) meet requirements for the WPS (see 8.3).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.3.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review on welding procedure and qualification records include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Obtain acceptable WPS(s) and PQR(s) for the work.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Qualify WPS(s) where required and witness qualification effort.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Qualify or re-qualify welders where required and witness a percentage of the welder qualifications.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.4 NDE Information<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Confirm the NDE examiner(s), NDE procedure(s) and NDE equipment of the inspection organization are acceptable
for the work.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.4.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>NDE information that should be reviewed includes the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) NDE examiners are properly certified for the NDE technique (see 4.6).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) NDE procedures are current and accurate.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Calibration of NDE equipment is current.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) NDE procedures and techniques specified are capable of achieving the required acceptance/rejection
requirements.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_21>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>W ELDING  P ROCESSES , I NSPECTION ,  AND  M ETALLURGY
13<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.4.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review on NDE information include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Identify and correct deficiencies in certifications and procedures.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Obtain calibrated equipment.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.5 Welding Equipment and Instruments<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Confirm welding equipment and instruments are calibrated and operable.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.5.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Items that should be reviewed on welding equipment and instruments include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Welding machine calibration is current.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Instruments such as ammeters, voltmeters, contact pyrometers, have current calibrations.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Storage ovens for welding consumables operate with automatic heat control and visible temperature indication.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.5.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review on welding equipment and instruments include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Confirm recalibration of equipment and instruments.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Confirm replacement of defective equipment and instruments.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.6 Heat Treatment and Pressure Testing<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Confirm heat treatment and pressure testing procedures and associated equipment are acceptable.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.6.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Heat treatment and pressure testing items that should be reviewed include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Heat treatment procedure is available and appropriate (see 10.6).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Pressure testing procedures are available and detail test requirements (see 9.11).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) PWHT equipment calibration is current.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Pressure testing equipment and gauges calibrated and meet appropriate test requirements.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.6.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review on heat treatment and pressure testing equipment and procedures include the
following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Identify and correct deficiencies in procedures.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Obtain calibrated equipment.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_22>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>14
API R ECOMMENDED  P RACTICE  577<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.7 Materials<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Ensure all filler metals, base materials, and backing ring materials are properly marked and identified and if required,
perform PMI to verify the material composition.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.7.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details that should be reviewed on materials used during welding include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Material test certifications are available and items properly marked (including backup ring if used; see 10.8).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Electrode marking, bare wire flag tags, identification on spools of wire, etc. as-specified (see 9.2).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Filler material markings are traceable to a filler material certification.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Base metal markings are traceable to a material certification.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Recording of filler and base metal traceability information is performed.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>f)
Base metal stampings are low stress and not detrimental to the component.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>g) Paint striping color code is correct for the material of construction.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>h) PMI records supplement the material traceability and confirm the material of construction (see 9.2).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.7.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review on materials used during welding include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Reject non-traceable or improperly marked materials.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Reject inappropriate materials.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.8 Weld Preparation<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Confirm weld preparation, joint fit-up, and dimensions are acceptable and correct.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.8.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details related to weld preparation that should be reviewed include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Weld preparation surfaces are free of contaminants and base metal defects such as laminations and cracks.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Preheat, if required, applied for thermal cutting.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Hydrogen bake-out heat treatment, if required, performed to procedure.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Weld joint is free from oxide and sulfide scales, hydrocarbon residue, and any excessive build-up of weld-through
primers.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Weld joint type, bevel angle, root face, and root opening are correct.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>f)
Alignment and mismatch is correct and acceptable.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_23>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>W ELDING  P ROCESSES , I NSPECTION ,  AND  M ETALLURGY
15<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>g) Dimensions of base materials, filler metal, and weld joint are correct.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>h) Piping socket welds have proper gap.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.8.2 Potential Inspector Action<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Reject material or correct deficiencies.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.9 Preheat<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Confirm the preheat equipment and temperature.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.9.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Preheat equipment and preheat temperature that should be reviewed include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Preheat equipment and technique are acceptable.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Preheat coverage and temperature are correct (see 10.5).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Reheat, if required, applied to thermal cutting operations.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Preheat, if required, applied to remove moisture.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.9.2 Potential Inspector Action<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Identify and correct deficiencies in the preheat operations.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.10 Welding Consumables<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Confirm electrode, filler wire, fluxes, and inert gases are as specified and acceptable.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.2.10.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details related to welding consumables that should be reviewed include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Filler metal type and size are correct per procedure.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Filler metals are being properly handled and stored (see 7.7).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Filler metals are clean and free of contaminants.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Coating on coated electrodes is neither damaged nor wet.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Flux is appropriate for the welding process and being properly handled.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>f)
Inert gases, if required are appropriate for shielding and purging.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>g) Gas composition is correct and meets purity requirements.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>h) Shielding gas and purging manifold systems are periodically bled to prevent back filling with air.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>4.3 Tasks During Welding Operations<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Welding inspection during welding operations should include audit parameters to verify the welding is performed to
the procedures. Such tasks may include the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.1 Quality Assurance<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Establish a quality assurance and quality control audit procedure with the welding organization.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.1.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details on welding quality assurance and quality control audit procedures that should be reviewed include the
following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Welder is responsible for quality craftsmanship of weldments.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Welder meets qualification requirements.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Welder understands welding procedure and requirements for the work.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Special training and mock-up weldments performed if required.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Welder understands the inspection hold-points.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.1.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review related to welding quality assurance and quality control audit procedures include<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Review welder performance with welding organization.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) See Annex B.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.2 Welding Parameters and Techniques<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Confirm welding parameters and techniques are supported by the WPS and WPQ.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.2.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details related to welding parameters and welding technique that should be reviewed includes the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Essential variables are being met during welding.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>1) Filler material, fluxes, and inert gas composition/flow rate.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>2) Purge technique, flow rate, O 2  analysis, etc.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_25>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>W ELDING  P ROCESSES , I NSPECTION ,  AND  M ETALLURGY
17<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3) Rod warmers energized or where rod warmers are not employed, the welder complies with maximum exposure
times out of the electrode oven.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4) Preheating during tack welding and tack welds removed (if required).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>5) Welding technique, weld progression, bead overlap, etc.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>6) Equipment settings such as amps, volts, and wire feed.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>7) Preheat and interpass temperatures. As   detailed in API 582, the maximum interpass temperature should be
specified for austenitic stainless steels, duplex stainless steels, and non-ferrous alloys (i.e. Type-300 stainless
steels). The maximum interpass temperature should also be specified for carbon/low alloy steels that require
impact testing.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>8) Travel speed (key element in heat input).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>9) Heat input (where appropriate).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Mock-up weldment which simulates the production weld joint, meets requirements of the welding engineer and is
used to demonstrate welder capability or weld procedure parameters as required.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Welder adheres to good welding practices.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.2.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review related to welding parameters and welding technique includes the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Review mock-up weldment problems with welding engineer.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Review weld quality with welding organization.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) See Annex B.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.3 Weldment Examination<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Complete physical checks, visual examination, and in-process NDE.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.3.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details related to examination of the weldment that should be reviewed includes the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Tack welds to be incorporated in the weld are of acceptable quality.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Weld root has adequate penetration and quality.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Cleaning between weld passes and of back-gouged surfaces is acceptable.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Additional NDE performed between weld passes and on back-gouged surfaces shows acceptable results.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) In-process rework and defect removal is accomplished.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>f)
In-process ferrite measurement, if required, is performed and recorded.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>g) Final weld reinforcement and fillet weld size meets work specifications and drawings.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>4.3.3.2 Potential Inspector Action<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Reject unacceptable workmanship.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.4 Tasks Upon Completion of Welding<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Final tasks upon completion of the weldment and work should include those that assure final weld quality before

<<HEADING level=3>>4.4.1 Appearance and Finish<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Verify postweld acceptance, appearance and finishing of the welded joints.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.4.1.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details related to weld appearance and finish that should be reviewed includes the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Size, length and location of all welds conform to the drawings/specifications/code.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) No welds added without approval.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Dimensional and visual checks of the weld don’t identify welding discontinuities, excessive distortion, and poor
workmanship.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Temporary attachments and attachment welds removed and blended with base metal.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Discontinuities reviewed against acceptance criteria for defect classification.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>f)
PMI of the weld, if required, indicating compliance with the specification.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>g) Welder stamping/marking of welds confirmed.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>h) Perform field hardness check (see 9.10).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.4.1.2 Potential Inspector Actions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Inspect rework of   existing welds, removal of   welds and weld repairs made   as required.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.4.2 NDE Review<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Verify NDE is performed at selected locations and review examiner’s findings.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.4.2.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details related to NDE that should be reviewed includes the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Specified locations examined.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Specified frequency of examination.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) NDE performed after final PWHT.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Work of each welder included in random examination techniques.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>

<<PARAGRAPH>>f)

<<PARAGRAPH>>g) Documentation for all NDE correctly executed (see 9.11).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>4.4.3 Postweld Heat Treatment<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Verify postweld heat treatment is performed to the procedure and produces acceptable results.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.4.3.1 Quality Control Items To Assess<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Details related to postweld heat treatment that should be reviewed includes the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Paint marking and other detrimental contamination removed.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Temporary attachments removed.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Machined surfaces protected from oxidation.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Equipment internals, such as valve internals, removed to prevent damage.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Equipment supported to prevent distortion.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>f)
Thermocouples fastened properly.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>g) Thermocouples adequately monitor the different temperature zones and thickest/thinnest parts in the fabrication.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>h) Temperature monitoring system calibrated.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>i)
Local heating bandwidth is adequate.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>j)
Insulation applied to the component where required for local heating.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>k) Temperature and hold time are correct.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>l)
Heating rate and cooling rate are correct.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>m) Distortion is acceptable after completion of the thermal cycle.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>n) Hardness indicates an acceptable heat treatment (see 10.7).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Inspection reports are complete, accepted and signed by responsible parties.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Inspection reports, NDE examiners interpretations and findings are accurate (see 9.11).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<PARAGRAPH>>Actions for the inspector to review related to the inspection dossier includes the following.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Require additional inspection verifications to address deficiencies in findings.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>4.5 Non-Conformances and Defects<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.5.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>At any time during the welding inspection, if defects or non-conformances to the specification are identified, they
should be brought to the attention of those responsible for the work or corrected before welding proceeds further.
Defects should be completely removed and re-inspected following the same tasks outlined in this section until the
weld is found to be acceptable. Corrective action for a non-conformance depends upon the nature of the non-
conformance and its impact on the properties of the weldment. Corrective action may include reworking the weld. See
9.1 for common types of discontinuities or flaws that can lead to defects or non-conformances.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.5.2 Repair Welds<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>When inspection identifies a   rejectable defect, the inspector should   mark the area for repair, the defect removed, and
any necessary repair welding performed. Any repair welding should be performed according to a procedure accepted
by the inspector or engineer. After the repair, the weld should be reinspected. If the inspection indicates that the repair
is acceptable, no further action is taken, and the equipment/piping is placed into service. If the inspection indicates
that the defect was not removed or that a new defect is present, the repair weld is rejected and a second repair is
undertaken. After the second unsuccessful attempt at weld repair, the inspector and/or welding engineer should
evaluate the reason for the inadequacy of the weld repair.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>There are many factors that come into play when trying to determine the number of times a welded joint can
continuously be repaired before a complete cut-out of the weld is required such as: base metal material, complexity of
the weld configuration/position (e.g. furnace tubes or boiler tubes), or size of the weld. The welding engineer or
inspector should be notified when a weld has failed a weld quality test more than three times to help determine the
cause(s) of the defect(s) and the appropriate path forward.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.6 NDE Examiner Certification<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4.6.1  The referencing codes or standards may require the examiner be qualified in accordance with a specific code
and certified as meeting the requirements. Typically weld construction standards such as ASME for pressure vessels
or piping, and API 510 for in-service pressure vessel examination reference ASME Section V, Article 1, which when
specified by the referencing code, requires NDE personnel be qualified with one of the following:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) ASNT SNT-TC-1A,<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) ANSI/ASNT CP-189.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>These references give the employer guidelines (SNT-TC-1A) or standards (CP-189) for the certification of NDE
inspection personnel. They also require the employer to develop and establish a written practice or procedure that
details the employer’s requirements for certification of inspection personnel. It typically includes the training, and
experience prerequisites prior to certification, and recertification requirements. A certification scheme in accordance
with ISO 9712 may be specified for international work. ISO 9712 outlines certification quidelines generally organized
under a national scheme and vested in the individual. In the USA the scheme is managed by ASNT as the ACCP<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_30>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>22
API R ECOMMENDED  P RACTICE  577<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>(ASNT Central Certification Program). Although an Inspection company’s Written Practice may allow the employer to
appoint a Level III, the owner user may prefer that, at least for initial certification, a Level III Examiner be certified by
examination.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4.6.2  If the referencing code does not list a specific standard to be qualified against, qualification may involve
demonstration of competency by the personnel performing the examination or other requirements specified by the
owner-user. The API in-service inspection documents go further than this and for a number of specific circumstances
such as fitness-for-service (FFS) and welds not subject to hydrotest may require the use of personnel who have
passed a performance test such as the API QUTE (Qualification of Ultrasonic Examiners) or owner-user accepted
equivalent.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.6.3  Equivalency is determined by the relevant API committee and is posted on the API website. In general it is
defined as:<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Ultrasonic shear wave operators should be subject to a performance demonstration test that should meet or exceed
as a minimum the test protocols, criteria and passing scores described as follows.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) The test should be administered either by the owner-user or an independent third party as designated by the
owner-user. All testing protocols including design, manufacture, and verification of test samples should be
documented and retained under close limited supervision to ensure the test protocols remain confidential.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Candidates prior to performance testing should demonstrate training and certification to a national or international
certification scheme acceptable to the owner-user (see SNT-TC-1A, CP-189, EN473, or ISO 9712 for guidance).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Candidates should be provided with a written outline protocol which they should read and acknowledge prior to
commencement of the test.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) As a minimum the test should   comprise:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>1) Carbon steel (P1) plates  1 / 2  in. (12 mm) and 1 in. (25 mm) thick with a weld with a single or double‘V’ weld prep.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>2) Two carbon steel (P1) pipes 12 in. (300 mm) and 8 in. (200 mm) NPS, in the wall thickness range  1 / 2  in. to  3 / 4  in.
(12 mm to 17 mm).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>3) The samples should provide a weld length such that the total weld length examined by the candidate   should not
be less than 77 in. (1956 mm) in total.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4) The total weld length should   include a number of individual flaws simulating the following typical weld
imperfections:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>i)
lack of side wall fusion;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>ii)
lack of root fusion;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>iii) linear inclusions (slag);<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>iv) cracks;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>v) porosity.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Flaws   should be designed and placed so as to determine the candidate’s ability to detect and characterize a flaw,
and to accurately locate the flaw in relationship to the weld. Also, the individual should demonstrate the ability to
discern geometric indications like mismatch and weld root from actual flaws.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_31>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>W ELDING  P ROCESSES , I NSPECTION ,  AND  M ETALLURGY
23<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4.6.4  In order to be successful in the test, candidates should detect, characterize and locate 80 % of the known
flaws in the weld sections they have been requested to examine. Candidates who make more than 20 % overcalls i.e.
misinterpreting a geometric reflector as a flaw should   not be deemed to have passed the test.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4.6.5  Candidates   should be advised if they have passed or failed the test. No other data should be made available
in order to ensure the confidentiality of data relating to flaw, numbers, locations, types, and sizes.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4.6.6  The approval test should typically be valid for a period of three years after which the candidate should be
retested. If at any time the performance of an operator is called into question, the operator may be re-tested at the
owner-users discretion.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>4.6.7  Approval of any candidate under this protocol is restricted to the specific owner-user administering the test
and it should be utilized for compliance with the referenced paragraphs in API 510 and API 570 and should not be
deemed as an API certification or endorsement in any form.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>4.7 Safety Precautions<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Inspectors should be aware of the hazards associated with welding and take appropriate steps to prevent injury while
performing inspection tasks. As a minimum, the site’s safety rules and regulations should be reviewed as applicable
to welding operations. Hazards that the inspector would more commonly encounter in the presence of welding
include arc radiation, air contamination, airborne debris, tripping hazards (cables), and heat. The arc is a source of
visible, ultraviolet and infrared light. As such, eye protection using proper filters and proper clothing to cover the skin
should be used. Proper ventilation is necessary to remove air-borne particulates, which include vaporized metals. In
areas of inadequate ventilation, filtered breathing protection may be required. The use of gas-shielded processes in
confined spaces can create an oxygen deficient environment. Ventilation practice in these instances should be
carefully reviewed. Welding can produce sparks and other air-borne debris that can burn the eyes. Appropriate
precautions are necessary.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5 Welding Processes<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The inspector should understand the basic arc welding processes most frequently used in the fabrication and repair of
refinery and chemical process equipment. These processes include shielded metal arc welding (SMAW), gas
tungsten arc welding (GTAW), gas metal arc welding (GMAW), flux cored arc welding (FCAW), submerged arc
welding (SAW), stud welding (SW), plasma arc welding (PAW), and electrogas welding (EGW). Descriptions of less
frequently used welding process are available in the referenced material. Each process has advantages and
limitations depending upon the application and can be more or less prone to particular types of discontinuities.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.2 Shielded Metal Arc Welding (SMAW)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.2.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>SMAW is the most widely used of the various arc welding processes. SMAW uses an arc between a covered
electrode and the weld pool. It employs the heat of the arc, coming from the tip of a consumable covered electrode, to
melt the base metal. Shielding is provided from the decomposition of the electrode covering, without the application of
pressure and with filler metal from the electrode. Either alternating current (ac) or direct current (dc) may be
employed, depending on the welding power supply and the electrode selected. A constant-current (CC) power supply
is preferred. SMAW is a manual welding process. See Figure 1 and Figure 2 for schematics of the SMAW circuit and
welding process.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>5.2.2 Electrode Covering<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Depending on the type of electrode being used, the covering performs one or more of the following functions.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Provides a gas to shield the arc and prevent excessive atmospheric contamination of the molten filler metal.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Provides scavengers, deoxidizers, and fluxing agents to cleanse the weld and prevent excessive grain growth in
the weld metal.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Establishes the electrical characteristics of the electrode, stabilizes the welding arc and influences operability in
various welding positions.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Provides a slag blanket to protect the hot weld metal from the air and enhances the mechanical properties, bead
shape, and surface cleanliness of the weld metal.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) Provides a means of adding alloying elements to produce appropriate weld metal chemistry, mechanical
properties and increase deposition efficiency.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.2.3 Advantages of SMAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Some commonly accepted advantages of the SMAW process include:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Equipment is relatively simple, inexpensive, and portable.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Process can be used in areas of limited access.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Process is less sensitive to wind and draft than other welding processes.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Process is suitable for most of the commonly used metals and alloys.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.2.4 Limitations of SMAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Limitations associated with SMAW are:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Deposition rates are lower than for other processes such as GMAW.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Slag usually must be removed from every deposited weld pass,   at stops and starts, and before depositing a weld
bead adjacent to or onto a previously deposited weld bead.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.3 Gas Tungsten Arc Welding (GTAW)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.3.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>GTAW is an arc welding process that uses an arc between a non-consumable tungsten electrode and the weld pool.
The process is commonly referred to as TIG (tungsten inert gas) or heliarc welding, and is used with a shielding gas
and without the application of pressure. GTAW can be used with or without the addition of filler metal. The constant
current (CC) type power supply can be either dc or ac, and depends largely on the metal to be welded. Direct current
welding is typically performed with the electrode negative (DCEN) polarity. DCEN welding offers the advantages of
deeper penetration and faster welding speeds. Alternating current provides a cathodic cleaning (sputtering) that
removes refractory oxides from the surfaces of the weld joint, which is necessary for welding aluminum and
magnesium. The cleaning action occurs during the portion of the ac wave, when the electrode is positive with respect
to the work piece. See Figure 3 and Figure 4 for schematics of the GTAW equipment and welding process.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>5.3.2 Advantages of GTAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Some commonly accepted advantages of the GTAW process include:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) produces high purity welds, generally free from defects;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) little postweld cleaning is required;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) allows for excellent control of root pass weld penetration;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) can be used with or without filler metal, dependent on the application.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.3.3 Limitations of GTAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Limitations associated with GTAW process are:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) deposition rates are lower than the rates possible with consumable electrode arc welding processes;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) has a low tolerance for contaminants on filler or base metals;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) difficult to shield the weld zone properly in drafty environments.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>Figure 4—GTAW Welding<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4 Gas Metal Arc Welding (GMAW)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>GMAW is an arc welding process that uses an arc between continuous filler metal electrode and the weld pool. The
process is used with shielding from an externally supplied gas and without the application of pressure. GMAW may be
operated in semiautomatic, machine, or automatic modes. It employs a constant voltage (CV) power supply, and uses
either the short circuiting, globular, spray, or pulsed transfer modes to transfer metal from the electrode to the work.
The type of transfer is determined by a number of factors. The most influential are:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) magnitude and type of welding current;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) electrode diameter;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) electrode composition;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) electrode extension or contact tube-to-work distance (often referred to as “stick out”);<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) shielding gas.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>S ee Figure 5 and Figure 6 for schematics of the GMAW equipment and welding process.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

29<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.2 Short Circuiting Transfer (GMAW-S)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.2.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>GMAW-S encompasses the lowest range of welding currents and electrode diameters associated with the GMAW
process. This process produces a fast freezing weld pool that is generally suited for joining thin sections, out-of-
position welding, or root passes. Due to the fast-freezing nature of this process, there is potential for lack of sidewall
and interpass fusion when welding thick-wall equipment or a nozzle attachment.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.2.2 GMAW-MSC (Modified Short Circuit)<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The modified short-circuit GMAW process, designated the GMAW-MSC process, has several proprietary derivatives
of the short-circuiting transfer mode which use a modified waveform to reduce some of the problems found with short-
circuiting—mainly, spatter and a turbulent weld pool. Typically these systems sense the progression of the short
circuit as it happens and modulates the current to limit the amount of force behind spatter and turbulence-producing
events. GMAW-MSC power sources are software-driven to maintain optimum arc characteristics by closely
monitoring and controlling the electrode current during all phases of the short-circuit. There are a limited number of
companies that manufacture welding power supplies which employ this technology.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The GMAW-MSC process minimizes the disadvantages of GMAW-S while maintaining comparable weld metal
deposition rates and achieving X-ray quality welds. The welding process has the capability to complete open root
welds more rapidly than GTAW, with low heat input and no lack of fusion. The lower heat input results in smaller heat
affected zones (HAZ) as well as reduced distortion and chance of burn-through. The process appears to be more
tolerant of less experienced welders since GMAW-MSC is tolerant of gaps and capable of automatically maintaining
the optimum wire feed speed and contact tip-to-work distance, and allows the use of larger diameter GMAW wires.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.3 Globular Transfer<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The advantages of this transfer method are its low cost when carbon dioxide is used as a shielding gas, and a high
deposition rate. The maximum deposition rate for the globular arc transfer mode is about 250 in./min (110 mm/sec).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The globular arc transfer mode is often considered the least desirable of the GMAW variations due to the tendency to
produce high heat, a poor weld surface, and weld spatter or a cold lap. This process uses relatively low current (below
250 A). During welding, a ball of molten metal from the electrode tends to build up on the end of the electrode, often in
irregular shapes, with a diameter up to twice that of the electrode. When the droplet finally detaches (i.e. by gravity or
short circuiting) and falls to the work piece, it produces an uneven surface and weld spatter. The welding process
produces a high amount of heat and forces the welder to use a larger electrode wire. This increases the size of the
weld pool, and causes greater residual stresses and distortion in the weld area. The welding process uses carbon
dioxide as the shielding gas, and is limited to the flat and horizontal position.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.4 Spray Transfer<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The spray arc transfer mode results in a highly directed stream of discrete drops that are accelerated by arc forces.
Since these drops are smaller than the arc length, short circuits do not occur and the amount of spatter generated is
negligible. The inert gas shield allows the spray arc transfer mode to weld most metals. However, using this process
on materials thinner than about 0.250 in. (6.4 mm) may be difficult because of the high currents needed to produce
the spray arc. The spray arc transfer mode produces high weld metal deposition rates. At high deposition rates, the
welding process may produce a weld metal pool that is too large to be supported by surface tension depending on the
electrode diameter, limiting the use of the welding process in the vertical or overhead position. Specially designed
power supplies have been developed to address the work thickness and welding position limitations. The maximum
deposition rate for spray arc transfer mode is about 150 in./min (60 mm/sec).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_38>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>30
API R ECOMMENDED  P RACTICE  577<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.5 Pulsed Transfer<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The pulsed arc GMAW method was developed to overcome the thickness and welding position limitations. Pulsed
GMAW welding is a variation of the GMAW process. The welding process uses:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>1) a low background/constant current to sustain the arc without providing enough energy to produce drops at the
tip of the wire; and<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>2) a superimposed/pulsing current with an amplitude greater than the transition current necessary for spray
transfer.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>During the pulsing portion of the current cycle, one or more drops are formed and transferred. The frequency and
amplitude of the pulses control the rate at which the wire melts. Pulsing makes the desirable features of spray arc
transfer available for joining sheet metals and welding in all positions. The maximum deposition rate for pulsed arc
transfer mode is about 200 in./min (85 mm/sec). The pulsed arc GMAW method requires a power source capable of
providing current pulses with a frequency between 30 pulses/sec and 400 pulses/sec, and requires that the shielding
gas be primarily argon with a low carbon dioxide concentration.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.6 Advantages of GMAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Some commonly accepted advantages of the GMAW process include:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) the only consumable electrode process that can be used to weld most commercial metals and alloys;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) deposition rates are significantly higher than those obtained with SMAW;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) minimal postweld cleaning is required due to the absence of a slag.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.4.7 Limitations of GMAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Limitations associated with GMAW are:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) the welding equipment is more complex, more costly, and less portable than that for SMAW;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) the welding arc should be protected from air drafts that can disperse the shielding gas;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) when using the GMAW-S process, the weld is more susceptible to lack of adequate fusion.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.5 Flux Cored Arc Welding (FCAW)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.5.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>FCAW is an arc welding process that uses an arc between a continuous tubular electrode and the weld pool. The
process is used with shielding gas evolved from a flux contained within the tubular electrode, with or without additional
shielding from an externally supplied gas, and without the application of pressure. Normally a semiautomatic process,
the use of FCAW depends on the type of electrodes available, the mechanical property requirements of the welded
joints, and the joint designs and fit-up. The recommended power source is the dc constant-voltage type, similar to
sources used for GMAW. Figure 7 shows a schematic of FCAW equipment, while Figure 8 shows the welding process
with additional gas shielding. Figure 9 shows a schematic of the self-shielded FCAW process where no additional gas
is used.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>5.5.2 Advantages of FCAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Some commonly accepted advantages of the FCAW process include:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) The metallurgical benefits that can be derived from a flux.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Slag that supports and shapes the weld bead.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Higher deposition and productivity rates compared to other processes such as SMAW.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Shielding is produced at the surface of the weld that makes it more tolerant of stronger air currents than GMAW.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>Figure 9—FCAW Welding, Self-shielded<<HEADING_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_41>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>W ELDING  P ROCESSES , I NSPECTION ,  AND  M ETALLURGY
33<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.5.3 Limitations of FCAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Self-shielded FCAW is typically not recommended for pressure-containing welds. Limitations associated with FCAW
process are:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) Equipment is more complex, more costly, and less portable than that for SMAW.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) Self-shielding FCAW generates large volumes of welding fumes, and requires suitable exhaust equipment.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Slag should be removed between weld passes, and removed from surfaces planned for inspection. If a weld is
being placed in corrosive service, failure to remove slag from the weld cap or root can create sites for corrosion to
initiate.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) Backing material is required for root pass welding.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.6 Submerged Arc Welding (SAW)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.6.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Submerged arc welding is an arc welding process that uses an arc or arcs between a flux-covered bare metal
electrode(s) and the weld pool. The arc and molten metal are shielded by a blanket of granular flux, supplied through
the welding nozzle from a hopper. The process is used without pressure and filler metal from the electrode and
sometimes from a supplemental source (welding rod, flux, or metal granules). SAW can be applied in three different
modes: semiautomatic, automatic, and machine. It can utilize either a CV or CC power supply. SAW is used
extensively in shop pressure vessel fabrication and pipe manufacturing. Figure 10 shows a schematic of the SAW
process.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.6.2 Advantages of SAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Some commonly accepted advantages of the SAW process include:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) provides very high metal deposition rates;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) produces repeatable high quality welds for large weldments and repetitive short welds.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.6.3 Limitations of SAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Limitations associated with SAW are:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) a power supply capable of providing high amperage at 100   % duty cycle is recommended;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) weld is not visible during the welding process;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) equipment required is more costly and extensive, and less portable;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) process is limited to shop applications and flat position.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.7 Stud Arc Welding (SW)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.7.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>SW is an arc welding process that uses an arc between a metal stud or similar part and the work piece. Once the
surfaces of the parts are properly heated (i.e. when the end of the stud is molten and the work has an equal area of
molten pool), they are brought into contact by pressure. Shielding gas or flux may or may not be used. The process<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<PARAGRAPH>>a) process is primarily suitable for only carbon steel and low-alloy steels;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) process is specialized to a few applications.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>5.8 Plasma Arc Welding (PAW)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.8.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Plasma arc welding (PAW) is a variation of the gas tungsten arc welding (GTAW) process except that the tungsten
electrode is positioned within the body of the torch. There are two types of plasma arc welding, namely the transferred
arc process and non-transferred arc process. In the plasma transferred arc (PTA) process, similar to GTAW, the
workpiece is part of the electrical circuit, and the arc is struck between the tungsten electrode and the workpiece. By
constricting the arc, the plasma or ionized gas is forced through a fine-bore copper nozzle which constricts the arc,
and exits the orifice at high velocities (approaching the speed of sound). The process produces a collimated arc which
focuses the arc on a relatively small area of the workpiece with an arc temperature in excess of 20,000 °F
(11,093 °C). In the non-transferred arc process, also referred as the plasma spray (PS) process, the arc is struck
between the tungsten electrode and the constricting nozzle rather than with the work-piece. The plasma spray
process is solely used for the deposition of surface coatings. It is not used for making strength welds. A comparison of
the GTAW and PAW circuits and welding processes is shown in Figure 11.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>

<<HEADING level=3>>5.8.2 Advantages of PAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Some commonly accepted advantages of the PAW process include:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) arc has good tolerance to misalignments;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PAGE_END>>
<<PAGE_44>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>36
API R ECOMMENDED  P RACTICE  577<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) high welding rate;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) high penetrating capability (keyhole effect);<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>d) lower total heat input due to focused arc results in less distortion;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>e) welds produced are narrower than GTAW due to the constricted arc.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.8.3 Limitations of PAW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>The limitations of plasma arc welding include:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) expensive equipment;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) larger torch than GTAW makes access more difficult in narrow weld joints;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) Focused arc requires better control by the welder<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.9 Electrogas Welding (EGW)<<HEADING_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.9.1 General<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Electrogas welding (EGW) is similar to the GMAW process in that it a solid electrode can be fed into the joint.
Alternately, similar to the FCAW process in that a flux cored tubular electrode can be used. The weld is deposited in
the flat position with the molten metal continually being deposited at the bottom of the moving cavity. In electrogas
welding, fixed or moving metal (usually copper) or ceramic shoes (or dams), water cooled if needed, are set up before
starting, to bridge the gap between abutting plates and contain the molten metal until solidification is completed. The
welding process utilizes either a solid wire or flux-cored electrode. The weld area is protected from atmospheric
contamination by an externally supplied shielding gas, or by the gas produced by the disintegration of the flux-cored
electrode wire. Electrogas welding is used to make square-groove welds for butt and T-joints in the construction of
storage tanks, ship hulls and pressure vessels with plate thicknesses from  3 / 8  in. to 4 in. (10 mm to 100 mm). The
workpiece should be at least 0.4 in. (10 mm) thick, while the maximum thickness for one electrode is approximately
0.8 in. (20 mm). Additional electrodes make it possible to weld thicker workpieces. The height of the weld is limited
only by the mechanism used to lift the welding head. In general, it ranges from 4 in. (100 mm) to 50 ft (20 m).<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Low and medium carbon steels, low alloy high strength steels, and some stainless steels can be welded using the
electrogas process. Quenched and tempered steels may also be welded by the process, provided that the proper
amount of heat is applied. Figure 12 shows an electrogas welding set-up using a solid electrode.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<HEADING level=3>>5.9.2 Advantages of EGW<<HEADING_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>Some commonly accepted advantages of the EGW process include:<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>a) welding is usually done in one pass;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>b) very high deposition rates;<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
<<PARAGRAPH>>c) minimum distortion.<<PARAGRAPH_END>>
